iii | P a g e

Abstract
Surveying And Mapping, LLC conducted historical research and a marine archaeological survey of the
proposed 850-acre additional temporary workspace (ATWS) within Tabbs Bay associated with the Mont
Belvieu to Morgan’s Point Ethane Export 24-Inch Pipeline Project located in Harris County, Texas. Work
was completed between November 10 and November 21, 2014 and on February 6, 2015 under contract
with Atkins of Houston, Texas. The purpose of this work was to assess the proposed ATWS within Tabbs
Bay to be used for construction activities associated with the horizontal directional drill crossing
methods beneath portions of the Houston Ship Channel. Archaeological assessment of the ATWS
includes portions of Texas General Land Office Mineral Lease Tracts 12, 13, 17, 360, 361 and 362 in
upper Galveston Bay, all of which are designated by the Texas Historical Commission as having a high
probability for the occurrence of historic shipwrecks. The purpose of the study was to map potential
shipwrecks, so they can be avoided by bottom-disturbing activities associated with construction
activities occurring within the proposed ATWS. This study was mandated under Section 106 of the
National Historic Preservation Act (Public Law 89-665; 16 U.S.C. 470) and the Antiquities Code of Texas
(Texas Natural Resource Code, Title 9, Chapter 191). The minimum reporting and survey requirements
for marine archaeological studies conducted under a Texas Antiquities Permit are mandated by The
Texas Administrative Code, Title 13, Part 2, Chapters 26 and 28, respectively. Three unidentified
geophysical targets, Anomalies 1, 2 and 3, are recommended for avoidance because of their consistency
with anomalies reported over known shipwrecks. Two previously recorded prehistoric sites along the
Hog Island shoreline, 41HR682 and 41HR683, are recommended for avoidance due to the possibility that
portions of these two sites exist underwater in the ATWS.

iv | P a g e

I.

Introduction

Atkins contracted with Surveying And Mapping, LLC (SAM) in 2014 to conduct marine archaeological
investigations for the proposed additional temporary workspace (ATWS) within Tabbs Bay associated
with the Mont Belvieu to Morgan’s Point Ethane Export 24-Inch Pipeline Project. The proposed ATWS
will be used for the temporary placement of pipe strings, anchoring piles, and construction equipment
associated with horizontal directional drill (HDD) crossings of the Houston Ship Channel (HSC). Prior to
installation, a hydrostatic pretest will be conducted where portions of the pipe string will be submerged
and resting on the bottom of the bay floor.

13
17

12

362
362

361
361

360
360

Figure 1: Project Location (850 acres)
Marine archaeological survey was conducted in compliance both with Section 106 of the National
Historic Preservation Act (Public Law 89-665; 16 U.S.C. 470), which requires federally permitted projects
to give due consideration to cultural resources, and with the Antiquities Code of Texas (Texas Natural
Resource Code, Title 9, Chapter 191), which provides for the protection of cultural resources on state
lands. The minimum reporting and survey requirements for marine archaeological studies conducted
under a Texas Antiquities Permit are mandated by The Texas Administrative Code, Title 13, Part 2,
Chapters 26 and 28, respectively. The property surveyed is publicly owned and is under the control of
the Texas General Land Office (GLO). The project area (Figure 1) spans portions of GLO Mineral Lease
Tracts 12, 13, 17, 360, 361 and 362 in upper Galveston Bay all of which are designated by the Texas
Historical Commission (THC) as having a high probability for the occurrence of historic shipwrecks.
The archaeological survey was completed between November 10 and November 21, 2014. Shoal areas,
inaccessible during the November field session, were surveyed on February 6, 2015. No artifacts were
collected during this survey. The purpose of this work was to assess areas where the bay floor might be
disturbed by construction-related activities. All navigable work areas were surveyed to support an
archaeological assessment of the project by mapping potential shipwrecks, which may be eligible for
nomination to the National Register of Historic Places (NRHP), so they can be avoided by bottom1|Page

disturbing activities. In this area, there is also potential for submerged prehistoric sites, due to the
subsidence of land during historic times. Three unidentified magnetic anomalies are recommended for
avoidance because of their consistency with anomalies reported over known shipwrecks. Two previously
recorded prehistoric sites are also recommended for avoidance, because they may be partially
submerged in the survey area as a result of subsidence.
This report is organized into five sections that provide the necessary context for interpreting the results
of the archaeological survey. Section II summarizes the maritime background of upper Galveston Bay,
including its history, a synopsis of previous archaeological investigations in the vicinity, and the potential
for occurrence and preservation of archaeological sites in the study corridor. Section III describes the
methods used for acquisition and analysis of geophysical data. Section IV presents the results and
archaeological recommendations of this study. Finally, a list of all bibliographic references cited in the
text is provided as Section V. Appendix A (not for public disclosure) contains maps of the survey results.
Appendix B contains a table of targets and sites recommended for avoidance, including their geographic
coordinates (not for public disclosure). Appendix C contains a copy of Antiquities Permit 7060 and the
Antiquities Permit Application.

II.

Maritime Background

History
The focus of this section is to briefly summarize the broad historical events that would have affected the
volume of maritime traffic and, therefore, of maritime losses in the Tabbs Bay area.
The area known today as Tabbs Bay was the mouth of the San Jacinto River, prior to 1869 when the
artificial channel through Morgan’s Point was begun. Consequently, all navigation between Galveston
Bay and points upstream, such as Buffalo Bayou, had to pass through what is now the ATWS project
area. Sediment deposited by the river, as its waters entered the bay and slowed down, formed a small
deltaic feature known as Clopper’s Bar slightly south of the ATWS (United States [U.S.] Coast Survey
1852). This bar was both an obstacle and a hazard to navigating between the river and the bay.
Navigation upriver became a priority around the time Houston was founded by the Allen brothers in
1836. They began to clear snags and obstructions from Buffalo Bayou as early as the 1840s. In 1869 the
Buffalo Bayou Ship Channel Company attempted to create a channel across Galveston Bay; however,
financial troubles led to the company’s acquisition by Charles Morgan. Under Morgan’s ownership, the
channel through Morgan’s Point was completed in the early 1870s, redirecting the flow of the San
Jacinto River and northbound traffic away from the ATWS project area. Morgan built a shipping terminal
at Clinton during this period. The channel leading from Morgan’s Point to Clinton was deepened to 12
feet (ft) by 1874, and in 1876 the first cargo arrived at the Clinton Terminal (Gearhart 1999: 6).
Vessel traffic too deep for Clopper’s Bar could bypass the mouth of the San Jacinto River through the
new cut at Morgan’s Point. Shallow-draft traffic likely continued to utilize the old river route across what
is now Tabbs Bay, especially vessels traveling between Cedar Bayou and Houston, or those seeking to
2|Page

avoid the toll charged by Morgan for passage on his ship channel until his death in 1878 when the
channel was sold to the federal government (Alperin 1977; Foster et al. 1993: 48-49).
Development of Cedar Bayou, near Tabbs Bay, began in the early 19th century. Citizens of Cedar Bayou
petitioned to join the Austin Colony in 1827 (Jones et al. 2009: 18-19). By 1850 there were 40 families
living along Cedar Bayou in the vicinity of Baytown. Navigation along Cedar Bayou was established by
1854. Ranching and agriculture were the primary economic focus of the area until after the Civil War
when brick making became popular. Commercial navigation to Galveston from Cedar Bayou likely
increased at this time in order to ship bricks to market (Henson 1986: 64, cited in Jones et al. 2009).
Houston was not a major destination for Cedar Bayou bricks, partly because Houston had
its own brick-making industry and partly because laden sailboats had difficulty
navigating the narrow channel to Houston. Non-powered vessels could be towed up the
channel, but at a substantial cost. The wide-open bay made transport to Galveston much
easier and more profitable. By necessity, Cedar Bayou merchants used shallow-draft
sailing craft almost exclusively. Steamboat traffic in the area had all but ceased once the
Houston to Galveston Railroad was established in 1860 (which also forced brick makers
to keep their prices competitively low). The typical Cedar Bayou craft was a flatbottomed sloop between 25 and 35 ft long and 12-ft beam. They had an aft cabin and a
decked-over forward section to protect cargo. Most were single-masted and rigged with a
main, jib, and sometimes spinnaker sails. Two-masted centerboard schooners were also
used, though they were less prevalent. In addition to bricks, these vessels hauled cotton,
livestock, and agricultural products. Many of the boats were built by local shipwrights;
others came from Lake Charles, Louisiana, and other Gulf coast ports (Henson 1986:66)
(Jones et al. 2009: 18-19).
The discovery of the Goose Creek oil field in 1908 created a local boomtown economy. By 1917, the first
refinery was built in the area at Baytown by the Humble Oil and Refining Company. The oil boom took
advantage of the newly opened deepwater HSC. In 1930 a channel 10 ft deep and 100 ft wide was
authorized from the HSC to a point 11 miles above the mouth of Cedar Bayou. Dredging of the bay
portion was completed by 1931 (Jones et al. 2009: 19).

Previous Investigations
A number of previous historic properties investigations have been conducted in the vicinity of the
present study. SAM reviewed the findings of previous cultural resource studies having relevancy to the
present project. Research was limited to the use of published sources.
In 1991, Espey, Huston & Associates (EH&A) completed an archaeological remote-sensing survey along
the HSC for the United States Army Corps of Engineers (USACE), Galveston District (Hoyt 1992). That
project’s objectives were to identify significant shipwrecks and other historical resources within
Galveston Bay that might be impacted by future USACE undertakings. The project’s survey corridor
measured over 40-miles long and included a linear corridor from the inner end of Bolivar Roads to the
Beltway 8 bridge crossing near Houston, as well as a 3.6-mile section of the Galveston Ship Channel.
That project resulted in a listing of over 3,400 magnetic anomalies and 570 sonar targets.

3|Page

EH&A completed extensive archival research for the USACE in 1992 (Foster et al. 1993), as a supplement
to the project reported by Hoyt (1992). Foster et al. defined areas of potential archaeological and
historical sites within and adjacent to the HSC. As a result of that research, 103 previously recorded
archaeological, historical, and shipwreck sites and over 600 other potential historic sites were reported
within 1 mile of the HSC. EH&A compiled that information into a database containing over 500 possible
shipwreck sites and made recommendations regarding particular sites and portions of the study area
warranting further research.
EH&A conducted archaeological diving at 23 locations along the HSC in 1993 including 4 targets along
the HSC in the vicinity of Hog Island near Tabbs Bay (Hoyt and Schmidt 1993). Figure 2 shows those 4
locations, labeled 4, 5, 19 and 21, west of Hog Island. Locality 4 was tentatively identified as remains of a
collapsed navigation range tower. No targets were ever successfully relocated at Locality 5; therefore,
no diving was conducted there. Locality 19 was attributed to modern debris, and the source of Locality
21 was not discovered (Hoyt and Schmidt 1993: 32, 37, 57-58 and 61).

Figure 2: Excerpt from Hoyt and Schmidt (1993:33, Figure 9) showing dive localities 4, 5, 19 and 21
investigated by EH&A archaeologists in 1993 near Hog Island
In 1994, EH&A undertook a series of cultural resource investigations for the USACE to study potential
cultural resource localities along the HSC (Hoyt et al. 1998). That project included, in part, a close-order
magnetometer survey of selected, previously defined areas along the HSC between the Beltway 8 Bridge
and the outer end of the Galveston jetties. Results of those investigations revealed 242 historic site
localities along the HSC. All localities were identified by name, type, age, potential archaeological
integrity, potential NRHP eligibility, and research priority status. In addition, several hundred magnetic
anomalies were identified during the remote-sensing survey. Based on a correlation of anomalies with
4|Page

suspected historic site localities, recommendations were made for field examination of potential sites
associated with the old Lynchburg town plat, the San Jacinto town plat, the industrial/commercial areas
on San Jacinto River (near Old River), the Bayport Channel area, Red Fish Bar, and the Galveston Jetties.
In June 1994 EH&A undertook archival research and remote-sensing survey of five Beneficial Use Areas
located in Galveston Bay (Hoyt et al. 1995). These investigations included the survey of the 1,650-acre
Atkinson Island Marsh located about three quarters of a mile south of the present study area. Seven
possible shipwreck locations were reported within that survey area from THC records at the time.
EH&A’s study recommended five targets as potentially associated with shipwreck remains, including one
associated with the visible mast of a fishing trawler protruding from the marsh.
In 1999, EHA conducted archival research and a remote-sensing survey of a section of Buffalo Bayou
east of the Interstate 610 East Bridge in Harris County. That work was performed for Eagle Asphalt
Products, Inc. in anticipation of construction of barge docking facilities (Gearhart 1999). The survey
identified three magnetic anomalies that could not be associated with extant structural features and
seven sonar targets. Only one anomaly, believed to be a submerged mooring dolphin, was determined
to be within the proposed area of impact. As the anomaly did not appear to represent a potentially
significant historic resource no further investigation was recommended. However, as the area was
determined to contain potentially significant historic resources it was recommended that impacts be
avoided shoreward of proposed construction and dredging.
In 2006, Panamerican Consultants Inc. performed a marine remote-sensing survey along the proposed
Osprey Lines, LLC container terminal for Lanier and Associates Inc. (Lydecker 2006). The survey
encompassed a 900-ft-long by 200-ft-wide area in Chambers County (adjacent to the Cedar Bayou M-3
survey area reported in Jones et al. 2009) and included magnetometer and side-scan sonar coverage.
Panamerican located no potentially significant cultural resources and recommended that the project
continue with no further investigations.
In October and November 2008, PBS&J conducted a multiphased cultural resources investigation for the
proposed improvements to the Cedar Bayou Navigation Channel in Chambers and Harris counties (Jones
et al. 2009). The investigations consisted of a marine remote-sensing survey, an intensive terrestrial
survey of two bend easings and two mitigation areas, a shoreline survey by boat, and the relocation and
assessment of five previously recorded prehistoric sites. The marine remote-sensing survey was
conducted in four discrete sections of Cedar Bayou between Ijams Lake and State Highway 146 and
encompassed approximately 79 acres. As a result of the marine investigations, six magnetic anomalies
and six sonar targets were selected as potential historic sites, and their avoidance by the proposed
dredging was recommended. The surveys were conducted less than a mile east of the present study
area; however, investigations were limited to landlocked portions of the Cedar Bayou Channel and did
not extend into Galveston Bay.
In 2009, PBS&J conducted a marine remote-sensing survey for the Houston Ship Channel Project in
Chambers County, Texas on behalf of the USACE, Galveston District (Jones et al. 2010). The purpose of
the study was to identify any submerged historic sites that might be impacted by the creation of
5|Page

placement areas for dredged sediments and a Marsh Beneficial Use Site associated with channel
improvements. The project area lies east of Atkinson Island in the northwestern portion of Galveston
Bay. The study consisted of a magnetometer and side-scan sonar survey of a 1,300-acre area,
supplemented by hydraulic probing of selected targets. Four magnetic anomalies were identified as
possible buried shipwrecks and were selected for further investigation by hydraulic probing. Three
targets were eliminated by probing. A fourth target, AI-12, was investigated further by divers and
ultimately recommended for clearance from further consideration as a potential historic site.
In January 2010, PBS&J performed a cultural resource assessment of data collected during a marine
remote-sensing survey of Sneed Shipbuilding in Old River in preparation for construction of a proposed
dry dock facility in Channelview, Harris County. The project area lies along the north bank of Old River,
approximately 1.5 miles east of its junction with the San Jacinto River. The marine remote-sensing study
consisted of a magnetometer, side-scan sonar and bathymetric survey of the entire 14-acre project area.
The results of the investigation revealed no magnetic or acoustic anomalies suggestive of potential
submerged cultural resources within the project area (Tubby 2010).

Potential for Historic Shipwrecks
SAM researched potential shipwrecks reported within 2 miles of the project area (Table 1). Sources
consulted for this research included the Texas Archaeological Sites Atlas, maintained by the THC, the
National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration (NOAA) Automated Wreck and Obstruction
Information System (AWOIS) database, and a shipwreck database compiled by PBS&J (now Atkins). At
least 24 wrecks and 4 obstructions have been reported within a 2-mile radius of the project area,
excluding landlocked Cedar Bayou. Most of these are known either from wreck symbols on NOAA charts
or from AWOIS listings.
One or possibly two shipwrecks have been reported within the project area. The THC shipwreck
database lists two wrecks (THC #1324 from NOAA 11326, June 1978 edition and THC #1330 from NOAA
11328, June 1978 edition); however, a note in both entries suggests that they might represent the same
shipwreck. Two wreck symbols are shown near one another along the Galveston Bay shoreline of Tabbs
Bay on the January 2013 edition of NOAA Chart 11328. The easternmost of these symbols also is shown
on the 2012 edition of NOAA Chart 11326. The AWOIS database was searched for the years 2005 and
2008. Neither wreck was listed in AWOIS for those years. The PBS&J shipwreck database shows a single
unidentified wreck (PBS&J #487) reported at this location and lists the source of information as NOAA
Chart 11328. No additional information has been found, and no sign of wreckage was observed in the
field. It appears that at least one wreck had been reported at this location by 1970.

Factors Affecting Vessel Loss
The main factors influencing the loss of vessels in the survey corridor are the density of vessel traffic and
weather. Historic navigation to this area began by the mid-nineteenth century, probably decreased in
the early 1870’s when the cut through Morgan’s Point was created, and presumably increased with
discovery of the Goose Creek Oilfield in 1908. Stranding is the most common mechanism by which ships
are wrecked, accounting for about 64 percent of total losses reported by the U.S. Lifesaving Service for
the period 1876 through 1914 (Gearhart, et al. 1990: Volume IV, 59-61). Two thirds of stranding events
were caused by weather. Stranding also is the most likely source of shipwrecks in the survey corridor.
Severe weather was, and remains, the most significant environmental factor contributing to shipwrecks,
accounting for 55 percent of total losses reported by the U.S. Lifesaving Service for the period 1876
through 1914. Prior to modern weather forecasting, mariners were hampered by relatively short
warnings of approaching storms. Hazardous weather conditions can occur along the Texas Coast
throughout much of the year. Strong northern winds are generated through the area each winter by the
passage of 15 to 20 northers, or rapidly moving polar fronts, each generally lasting 24–36 hours. Such
storms may contain 50-mile-per-hour winds and generate intense wave activity and long shore currents
(McGowen 1976:19–23, 94). Hurricanes season lasts from late June through October. Hurricanes have
winds upwards of 74 miles per hour and can have devastating effects on any ships caught in their paths.
Factors Affecting Vessel Preservation
Preservation of vessels wrecked within the survey corridors will depend largely upon the composition of
their hull and the degree to which they become and remain buried. In this area, the lower portions of a
wrecked vessel can be expected to become buried soon after sinking due, in part, to a combination of
scour and liquefaction of sediments during storms and sedimentation during quiescent periods. Metal
vessels remain exposed much longer than wooden ones, although their lower hulls are equally
susceptible to burial. Any wooden components that are not buried tend to disintegrate relatively
quickly. Wood-boring organisms rapidly consume exposed wood. In shallow water, wood weakened by
biological organisms and water saturation is then disarticulated and laid flat or removed by hurricanes
8|Page

and fishing trawlers. In areas such as this, having copious quantities of weakly consolidated sediment,
significant portions of a wooden hull can become buried, protecting it from biological degradation.
Burial produces an oxygen-deprived environment, which promotes long-term preservation of wood.
Iron artifacts, on the other hand, corrode five times faster in seawater than when buried on land.
Calcium carbonate forms a concretion around iron artifacts that incorporates surrounding materials
such as rock and sand or even other artifacts. Oxidation can eventually leach out most or all of the iron,
leaving only a carbonate mold of the original artifact (Hamilton 1998).

Potential for Submerged Terrestrial Sites
Many areas upstream from Morgan’s Point have experienced significant subsidence over the past half
century or so. At the same time, other areas, including portions of Hog Island, have been enlarged by
placement of dredged material. Modern aerial photography of Tabbs Bay available on Google Earth,
dating from 1978 to present, was compared with a map drawn by the Army Corps of Engineers (USACE
1905) in order to estimate the degree and areas of shoreline change within the survey boundary. Based
on this comparison, it is clear that roughly the northern half of Hog Island, including about a quarter
mile of shoreline bordering the northwest corner of the survey area likely existed in 1852 (U.S. Coast
Survey 1852) and certainly existed by 1905. The southern half of the island appears to have been
created after 1905, as it occupies the former path of the San Jacinto River around the eastern end of
Morgan’s Point before the HSC was dredged through the point. The shoreline of Hog Island has not
changed significantly since at least 1978.
The northern shoreline of the survey area has not changed appreciably since at least 1970. This
shoreline is a high bank, which would have remained far above water despite subsidence that might
have occurred. Archaeological sites located on the top edge of this bank, could potentially have fallen
into the bay as the shoreline eroded; however, they would now be completely removed from their
original stratigraphic context.
The THC Site Atlas shows 11 terrestrial archaeology sites bordering the project area. A summary of each
site is provided in Table 2. Ten of those sites were reported as prehistoric Native American sites
characterized by shell midden deposits; however, one of those ten, Site 41HR681, has since been
reclassified as material redeposited by a dredge. Site 41HR408 is the historic home site of Dr. Ashbel
Smith. Seven sites are situated on top of a 16-ft-high (5-meter-high) cut bank on the northern project
boundary, thus subsidence is not an issue.
Only two sites, 41HR682 and 41HR683 located on the northern shore of Hog Island, appear likely to have
subsided. Both sites were originally recorded at sea level elevation within Exxon pipeline right-of-ways
by C.R. Ebersole and Sheldon Kindall. Neither site was considered potentially eligible for the NRHP by
Ebersole and Kindall; however, the THC has requested that both sites be recommended for avoidance
until such time as they are determined ineligible. Each site was reported as being at least 20 by 100 feet
in size. It is possible, due to subsidence, that these sites extend beyond their reported boundaries and
that portions may be submerged within the project area along the Hog Island shoreline. Extensive shoals
along this shore prevented acquisition of side-scan sonar data, so the results of this marine
9|Page

archaeological survey cannot provide corroborating evidence for the presence of submerged prehistoric
sites in the project area. In the event that any submerged shell midden deposits are exposed (not buried
in the bay bottom), they would most likely be heavily colonized by living oysters, since the project area
contains extensive living oyster populations.

Methodology

SAM’s survey methodology followed the requirements stipulated by the THC. The survey was conducted
from either an airboat or a 25-foot enclosed survey vessel, depending on weather conditions and water
depths. Instrumentation essential to the archaeological survey included an Edgetech 4125 side-scan
sonar (600-kHz), a Geometrics G882 marine magnetometer, an Odom CVM recording fathometer (200
kHz), and a Trimble Differential Global Positioning System (GPS). Hypack software was used for
10 | P a g e

navigation and for logging of GPS, magnetometer and bathymetry data on the survey boat. Horizontal
positions were based on the Texas State Plane Coordinate System (NAD83, South Central Zone. U.S.
feet). GPS positions were integrated into sonar imagery using Edgetech’s Discover Sonar Acquisition
Software. Primary transects were surveyed parallel to one another and spaced an average of 66 ft (20
meters) apart.
The magnetometer was the primary instrument relied upon for locating and recognizing potential
shipwreck sites, except in areas dominated by pipeline and well/platform anomalies. The side-scan
sonar imagery provided verification of magnetic anomaly sources above the mud line and in areas
where the magnetometer was ineffectual due to the presence of oil and gas infrastructure. Submerged
sites, if present, would be visible on the side-scan sonar record if exposed and would be located solely
based on magnetometer readings if buried. Bathymetry data was collected as a tool for planning future
field investigations, if required, but is not illustrated in this report. Vessels predating World War II tend
to be constructed of wood thus generally are not preserved above the seafloor. Nevertheless buried
portions of such sites can retain a high level of artifact preservation and historic integrity. No artifacts
were collected during this survey.
The magnetometer sensor was towed on the water surface behind the survey boat. The sensor height
above the bottom was generally less than 4 ft. The layback of the sensor was varied depending on the
boat used. Magnetometer data were acquired at 0.2-second intervals using the Geometrics G882
cesium magnetometer and were logged to the navigation computer along with calculated offset
positions for the magnetometer sensor. Magnetometer data illustrated in Appendix A has been thinned
to a 1-second interval between data points. The Hypack navigation software calculated and recorded
position estimates for the magnetometer and echo sounder in real-time. Line spacing, sample rate, and
vessel speed determined the resolution of magnetic and bathymetric data.
The side-scan sonar sensor was towed from the survey vessel’s side at a depth slightly lower than the
hull. The sonar was set to image the seafloor for a distance of 164 ft (50 meters) to either side of the
survey path. A mosaic image of the sonar data (Appendix A) was created using Chesapeake’s SonarWiz
software. The data were analyzed to locate and map objects projecting above the seafloor.
A Microsoft EXCEL® spreadsheet was used to process the magnetometer data prior to contouring. The
spreadsheet uses an algorithm to remove diurnal fluctuations. The difference between anomalous
values and the local background field results in a dataset where abnormally high and low magnetic
values (anomalies) are centered around zero (the ambient level). This process removes low frequency
trends from the dataset, such as those caused by diurnal variation, geologic gradients, or gradual
changes in water depth while leaving high-frequency variations (anomalies) intact. Diurnal processing
allows visual representation of the data that reflects the polarity of magnetic anomalies.
A contour map of the diurnally-corrected magnetometer data was prepared using Bentley’s Geopak®
digital terrain modeling software using a 5-nT contour interval (Appendix A). Relative magnetic
amplitudes between +5 nT and −5 nT are considered insignificant for purposes of marine archaeological

11 | P a g e

interpretations. The 0-nT contour level is omitted from the contour map to prevent a cluttered
appearance.

Interpretation of Magnetometer Data
The probable sources for most magnetic anomalies are relatively small items of modern ferrous debris.
Such anomalies tend to be more concentrated in proximity to modern navigation channels and in other
areas of heavy use. Ferromagnetic debris is common in such settings due to the frequency of
commercial and recreational boat traffic. Concentrations of magnetic anomalies also tend to occur in
the vicinity of industrial developments, petroleum wells, and pipelines.
The method use here to interpret unidentified magnetic anomalies is based upon resemblance to
verified shipwreck and debris anomalies as detailed in Gearhart (2011) and summarized below. The
author has assembled, over the past several years, a diverse collection of magnetic anomalies recorded
over 29 verified shipwrecks and many debris sources in an effort to characterize differences between
these two categories of magnetic sources. Shipwrecks included in this dataset encompass a wide variety
of hull compositions (wood, iron, steel, and concrete), propulsion systems (sail, steam-driven
paddlewheels and propellers, oil and diesel screws, and towed or pushed barges), ages (mid-sixteenth to
the mid-twentieth century), and depositional environments (harbor entrances, surf zones, beaches,
marsh, oyster reefs, open bay, and the Gulf of Mexico). These vessels also wrecked in various ways
including by stranding, foundering (sinking), boiler explosions, and burning. Some were even partially
demolished and/or salvaged after wrecking. Based on Gearhart’s (2011) sample, it is possible to
characterize shipwreck and debris anomalies in an empirical fashion.

Complexity
Many archaeologists have attempted to characterize the differences between shipwreck anomalies and
those associated with ferromagnetic debris. Shipwreck anomalies frequently have been described as
appearing “multicomponent” or “complex”, emphasizing the fact that shipwreck anomalies, in certain
contexts, may exhibit multiple dipoles in addition to having unpaired monopolar amplitudes. Garrison et
al. (1989: II, 223) summarized the more common methods used at the time for prioritizing anomalies.
Historic shipwreck anomalies were said to share the following characteristics: multiple amplitude peaks
of differing magnitudes spread over an area greater than 10,000 square meters (2.5 acres), gentle
gradients, and a linear association with anomalies on adjacent transects. Debris anomalies were
characterized as typically having single amplitude peaks covering an area of less than 10,000 square
meters, steep gradients, and no alignment of anomalies on adjacent lines.

Horizontal Dimensions
The horizontal size of an anomaly is referred to by many archaeologists as “duration” in reference to
how many seconds a magnetometer registers anomalous readings as it passes overhead in the water
column. Duration is often used by archaeologists as a tool for discriminating potentially significant
anomalies from those more likely to be caused by debris. For example, Linden and Pearson (2014) would
consider an anomaly significant if it has amplitude of at least 50 nT and a duration of 65 feet or more.
Duration obviously depends upon the velocity of the survey boat; therefore this author instead refers to
12 | P a g e

duration as anomaly width or area exceeding the +/− 5-nT amplitude level. The widths of historic woodhulled shipwrecks and debris anomalies overlap significantly, thus width (or duration) is not a very
useful tool for discriminating between the two categories of anomaly. Most debris anomalies tend to be
no wider than anomalies observed over wood-hulled sailing ships or wood-hulled steamboats; however,
within the category of shipwreck anomalies, there is a 15-fold difference in width between the smallest
wood-hulled sailing vessel and the largest steel tanker. In other words, when it comes to width alone,
anomalies of small wooden ships, which tend to be the most historic, look more like debris anomalies
and bear less resemblance to larger, especially ferrous, shipwrecks.
The most difficult shipwrecks to detect are the small, wooden, generally historic sites, precisely because
their anomalies are in the size range of many debris anomalies. The smallest verified shipwreck anomaly
known to this author (Figure 3) was recorded over the wreck of an early nineteenth-century sailing
vessel, Site 41CL92. The site was shown by divers to measure at least 23 x 52 feet (7 x 15.9 meters) and
is characterized by a large collection of concreted artifacts, iron bar stock, and pig iron ballast (Borgens
2004).
Separating small shipwreck anomalies from debris anomalies in the same size range is complicated and
cannot be done on the basis of size alone. Garrison et al. (1989: II, 223) characterized shipwreck
anomalies as typically being larger than 10,000 square meters; however, if that were the only criterion
used for selecting significant anomalies, all of the wood-hulled sailing ships and all but one of the woodhulled steamboat anomalies known to this author would be excluded. For example, Site 41CL92,
believed to be either a 67-ft New Orleans schooner Hannah Elizabeth that sank in 1835 or the Mexican
naval sloop General Bustamante that sank in 1830 (Borgens 2004), covers an area of only 1,580 square
meters (0.4 acre) out to the 5-nT contour.

Amplitude
Amplitude is even less useful as a tool for differentiating shipwrecks from debris due to the substantial
overlap between shipwreck and debris anomaly amplitudes. The peak-to-peak amplitude of shipwreck
anomalies from Gearhart (2011) averages 270 nT for wood-hulled sailing vessels (n=6); 5,020 nT for
wood-hulled machine-powered vessels (n=7); and 10,386 nT for iron- and steel-hulled vessels (n=12).
Debris anomalies can have amplitude virtually anywhere within the range of shipwreck anomalies.
Linden and Pearson (2014) are being conservative by recommending targets having amplitudes of at
least 50 nT as potentially significant. Anomaly amplitude correlates poorly with vessel size because
amplitude depends greatly upon the distance between the magnetometer and the magnetic source.
Anomalies with large amplitude can result from a wooden shipwreck containing a relatively small iron
mass when measured at close range.

Orientation
Despite the diversity of shipwrecks, their anomalies consistently have one characteristic in common with
one another. They are aligned closely with earth’s magnetic field (e.g., Figure 3). In mid-latitudes of the
northern hemisphere, where all of the wrecks discussed by Gearhart (2011) occur, the primary negative
pole of each shipwreck anomaly is located to the north of its positive pole. By contrast, the alignments
of debris anomalies are not limited to one particular orientation.
13 | P a g e

A simple debris source produces an anomaly dominated by its permanent magnetic field, thus the
anomaly alignment associated with a solitary item of debris is determined by the orientation of the
object on the seabed, and the polar axis of debris anomalies can be oriented along any point of the
compass. On the other hand, shipwrecks are complex anomaly sources comprised of many
ferromagnetic objects. The randomly-oriented permanent magnetic field of each shipwreck component
tends to be cancelled by the overlapping fields of other nearby sources. The weaker earth-induced field
of each source is reinforced by the induced fields of all other nearby components, since all of the
induced fields are oriented parallel to earth’s local field lines. As a result, shipwrecks and other complex
sources have anomalies closely aligned to the direction of magnetic north.

Figure 3: Example of a Verified Shipwreck Anomaly, Site 41CL92
The characteristic of anomaly orientation is used to differentiate magnetic anomalies caused by most
ferrous debris from anomalies caused by shipwrecks. Consideration of magnetic anomaly orientation
has potential to eliminate up to about 80 percent of debris anomalies from further archaeological
concern. By chance, about 20 percent of simple debris sources have anomaly orientations overlapping
those of complex sources, including shipwrecks. At present there is no consistent method for eliminating
that 20 percent of debris from consideration as a potential buried shipwreck.
14 | P a g e

Many anomalies, however, can be eliminated from consideration as potential shipwrecks simply by
demonstrating that their orientations differ substantially from the direction of magnetic north. The only
cases in which anomalies of simple debris sources should align with earth’s magnetic field is when, by
chance, their magnetic moments, as determined by their orientation on the seafloor, closely align with
magnetic north. The opposite case, a wood-hulled shipwreck with a magnetic anomaly not aligned with
magnetic north, is virtually impossible, as it would require a large percentage of a wreck’s many
ferromagnetic components, by chance, to have the same magnetic moment.
Interpretation of magnetic anomalies, in practice, was done by comparing the magnetometer data,
contoured at a 5-nT interval, to the anomaly of a small wood-hulled historic shipwreck, 41CL92, shown
in Figure 3. The verified 41CL92 shipwreck anomaly was contoured using the same parameters as the
survey data from this project and was properly oriented and scaled to match the data to which it was
compared. Any anomalies that could not have a similar polar orientation, assuming a sufficient data
density, were excluded from further consideration. Anomalies remaining as shipwreck candidates were
then researched with the Texas Railroad Commission to determine which ones could be attributed to
abandoned petroleum wells.

IV.

Conclusions

Results
The side-scan sonar and magnetometer data is illustrated in Appendix A. Archaeological assessment was
completed for portions of GLO Lease Tracts 12, 13, 17, 360, 361 and 362. Three unidentified geophysical
targets, Anomalies 1, 2 and 3 (Figures 4, 5 and 6; Appendices A and B), have magnetic anomalies

Figure 4: Anomaly 1
15 | P a g e

Figure 5: Anomaly 2

Figure 6: Anomaly 3
consistent with those reported over verified shipwrecks. Anomaly 1 is located adjacent a linear feature
labeled “Ruins” on the January, 2013 edition of NOAA Chart 11328. No potentially significant side-scan
sonar targets were observed.

16 | P a g e

Magnetic anomalies associated with several petroleum wells, numerous pipelines and an overhead
transmission line were also observed within the ATWS. The magnetometer data in many areas,
especially in the western third of the survey, is dominated by high-amplitude pipeline and well
anomalies, which effectively exclude large areas from detection of other buried magnetic sources,
including shipwrecks, should they be present. In such areas, one can only expect to detect wrecks
through the use of sonar but only if they are exposed above the seafloor. There is a narrow area along
both shorelines that could not be surveyed with either magnetometer or sonar due to extremely
shallow water. Sonar coverage also was limited over a few very shallow oyster reef areas, as indicated in
Appendix A.
There is potential for two prehistoric archaeological sites, 41HR682 and 41HR683 (Appendices A and B),
reported on the THC Site Atlas along the northern shore of Hog Island, to extend into submerged areas
of the ATWS. Both sites were originally recorded at sea level elevation within Exxon pipeline right-ofways by C.R. Ebersole and Sheldon Kindall. There is no report of their investigations on file with the
Texas Archaeological Research Laboratory. It is possible, due to subsidence, that these two sites extend
beyond their reported boundaries and that portions may be submerged within the project area along
the Hog Island shoreline. Neither site was considered potentially eligible for the NRHP by Ebersole and
Kindall; however, the THC has requested that these sites be avoided due to the possibility that intact
portions might be submerged in the project area.
Extensive shoals along this shore prevented acquisition of side-scan sonar data, so the results of this
marine archaeological survey cannot provide corroborating evidence for the presence of submerged
prehistoric sites in the project area. In the event that any submerged shell midden deposits are exposed
(i.e., not buried in the bay bottom), they would most likely be heavily colonized by living oysters, since
the project area contains extensive living oyster populations.

Recommendations
Three unidentified magnetic anomaly was observed that might be interpreted as a cultural resource.
Anomalies 1, 2, and 3 (Figures 4, 5, and 6; Appendices A and B) are recommended for avoidance. The
bay bottom should not be disturbed within 164 ft (50 meters) of their perimeters.
Two prehistoric sites, 41HR682 and 41HR683 (Appendices A and B), were independently reported along
the northern shore of Hog Island. These sites might extend into the bay due to modern subsidence. Both
sites are recommended for avoidance at the request of the THC. The bay bottom should not be
disturbed within 164 ft (50 meters) of either site’s perimeter.
There is potential for historically significant sites to remain undetected in marginal areas where 100percent survey coverage could not be achieved and where magnetic anomalies from pipelines, for
example, prevent the reliable detection of smaller isolated anomaly sources. In the event that any
shipwreck remains, or other potentially historic materials, are discovered during the course of
construction, work should be halted within 164 ft (50 meters) until the THC can provide direction
concerning the discovery.
